Task instruction sent to agent 
# Onboarding Task: Apache Kafka Codebase Orientation

You are a new engineer joining the Apache Kafka project. Your first task is to explore the codebase and answer the following orientation questions. This will help you understand the project structure, architecture, and development practices.

## Task

Explore the Apache Kafka repository and answer the following questions. Write your findings to `/logs/agent/onboarding.md` with numbered answers corresponding to each question.

## Questions

1. **Build System and Broker Startup**: What build system does Kafka use, and what is the main entry point for starting a Kafka broker? Describe the key classes involved in broker initialization.

2. **Module Structure**: Kafka is organized into multiple modules/directories. What are the core modules and what are their primary responsibilities? Explain how the codebase is structured.

3. **Topic Creation Flow**: How does topic creation work end-to-end in Kafka? Trace the path from when a client creates a topic through the broker's request handling to the actual topic creation. What are the key classes and methods involved?

4. **Testing Framework**: What testing frameworks and patterns does Kafka use? How would you write tests for Kafka components (both unit tests and integration tests)?

5. **Configuration System**: How are broker configuration parameters defined and managed in Kafka? Where is the configuration registry located and how are config values validated?

6. **Adding a New Broker Config**: If you needed to add a new broker configuration parameter, what files would you modify and what steps would you follow? Include details about validation, dynamic updates, and testing.

## Deliverable

Write your answers to `/logs/agent/onboarding.md` in the following format:

```
# Apache Kafka Codebase Orientation

## 1. Build System and Broker Startup
[Your detailed answer with specific file paths and class names]

## 2. Module Structure
[Your detailed answer describing modules and their responsibilities]

## 3. Topic Creation Flow
[Your detailed answer tracing the request flow]

## 4. Testing Framework
[Your detailed answer about testing patterns]

## 5. Configuration System
[Your detailed answer about config management]

## 6. Adding a New Broker Config
[Your detailed answer with step-by-step process]
```

Be specific in your answers - include file paths, class names, method names, and explain the architectural patterns you discover.

   172→  override def startup(): Unit = {
   173→    if (!maybeChangeStatus(SHUTDOWN, STARTING)) return
   174→    val startupDeadline = Deadline.fromDelay(time, config.serverMaxStartupTimeMs, TimeUnit.MILLISECONDS)
   175→    try {
   176→      sharedServer.startForBroker()
   177→
   178→      info("Starting broker")
   179→
   180→      val clientMetricsReceiverPlugin = new ClientMetricsReceiverPlugin()
   181→      config.dynamicConfig.initialize(zkClientOpt = None, Some(clientMetricsReceiverPlugin))
   182→
   183→      /* start scheduler */
   184→      kafkaScheduler = new KafkaScheduler(config.backgroundThreads)
   185→      kafkaScheduler.startup()
   186→
   187→      /* register broker metrics */
   188→      brokerTopicStats = new BrokerTopicStats(config.remoteLogManagerConfig.isRemoteStorageSystemEnabled())
   189→
   190→      quotaManagers = QuotaFactory.instantiate(config, metrics, time, s"broker-${config.nodeId}-")
   191→
   192→      logDirFailureChannel = new LogDirFailureChannel(config.logDirs.size)
   193→
   194→      metadataCache = MetadataCache.kRaftMetadataCache(config.nodeId, () => raftManager.client.kraftVersion())
   195→
   196→      // Create log manager, but don't start it because we need to delay any potential unclean shutdown log recovery
   197→      // until we catch up on the metadata log and have up-to-date topic and broker configs.
   198→      logManager = LogManager(config,
   199→        sharedServer.metaPropsEnsemble.errorLogDirs().asScala.toSeq,
   200→        metadataCache,
   201→        kafkaScheduler,
   202→        time,
   203→        brokerTopicStats,
   204→        logDirFailureChannel,
   205→        keepPartitionMetadataFile = true)
   206→
   207→      remoteLogManagerOpt = createRemoteLogManager()
   208→
   209→

## Apache Kafka Build System and Broker Startup Process - Analysis Report

### 1. BUILD SYSTEM

**Build Tool**: **Gradle** (with Gradle Enterprise integration)

**Key Build Files**:
- `/workspace/build.gradle` - Root build file (112KB, defines overall configuration)
- `/workspace/settings.gradle` - Settings file that includes all sub-modules
- `/workspace/gradle/dependencies.gradle` - Dependency definitions

**Module Structure** (from settings.gradle):
The build includes multiple modules organized as:
- `core` - Main server/broker implementation
- `server` - Server-specific Java classes (network, security, configuration)
- `server-common` - Shared server components
- `clients` - Client libraries
- `connect` - Kafka Connect framework
- `streams` - Kafka Streams
- Plus many others (tools, storage, raft, metadata, etc.)

**Build Configuration** (from build.gradle):
- Java version: 8+ (compatible with Java 8 and later)
- Gradle plugins include: Spotbugs, ScovCoverage, Shadow, Spotless
- Support for JDK 16+ with special JVM args for encapsulated internals
- Multiple test forks available with configurable parallelism

---

### 2. KAFKA BROKER ENTRY POINT

**Main Entry Point Class**:
```
/workspace/core/src/main/scala/kafka/Kafka.scala
```

**Main Method Flow**:
```
kafka.Kafka.main(args: Array[String])
  ├─ getPropsFromArgs() - Parse server.properties file
  ├─ buildServer() - Create appropriate server type:
  │  ├─ If requiresZookeeper: new KafkaServer()
  │  └─ Otherwise: new KafkaRaftServer()
  ├─ register LoggingSignalHandler (Unix signals)
  ├─ add shutdown hook
  ├─ server.startup() - Start the server
  └─ server.awaitShutdown() - Block until shutdown
```

```bash

137-usertool_resultRead
     1→Apache Kafka
     2→=================
     3→See our [web site](https://kafka.apache.org) for details on the project.
     4→
     5→You need to have [Java](http://www.oracle.com/technetwork/java/javase/downloads/index.html) installed.
     6→
     7→We build and test Apache Kafka with Java 8, 11, 17 and 21. We set the `release` parameter in javac and scalac
     8→to `8` to ensure the generated binaries are compatible with Java 8 or higher (independently of the Java version
     9→used for compilation). Java 8 support project-wide has been deprecated since Apache Kafka 3.0, Java 11 support for
    10→the broker and tools has been deprecated since Apache Kafka 3.7 and removal of both is planned for Apache Kafka 4.0 (
    11→see [KIP-750](https://cwiki.apache.org/confluence/pages/viewpage.action?pageId=181308223) and
    12→[KIP-1013](https://cwiki.apache.org/confluence/pages/viewpage.action?pageId=284789510) for more details).
    13→
    14→Scala 2.12 and 2.13 are supported and 2.13 is used by default. Scala 2.12 support has been deprecated since
    15→Apache Kafka 3.0 and will be removed in Apache Kafka 4.0 (see [KIP-751](https://cwiki.apache.org/confluence/pages/viewpage.action?pageId=181308218)
    16→for more details). See below for how to use a specific Scala version or all of the supported Scala versions.
    17→
    18→### Build a jar and run it ###
    19→    ./gradlew jar
    20→
    21→Follow instructions in https://kafka.apache.org/quickstart
    22→
    23→### Build source jar ###
    24→    ./gradlew srcJar
    25→
    26→### Build aggregated javadoc ###
    27→    ./gradlew aggregatedJavadoc
    28→
    29→### Build javadoc and scaladoc ###
    30→    ./gradlew javadoc
    31→    ./gradlew javadocJar # builds a javadoc jar for each module
    32→ 
... (truncated)
138-usertool_resultRead
     1→KRaft (Kafka Raft)
     2→==================
     3→KRaft (Kafka Raft) is a protocol based on the [Raft Consensus Protocol](https://www.usenix.org/system/files/conference/atc14/atc14-paper-ongaro.pdf)
     4→tailored for Apache Kafka.
     5→
     6→This is used by Apache Kafka in the KRaft (Kafka Raft Metadata) mode. We
     7→also have a standalone test server which can be used for performance testing. We describe the details to set this up below.
     8→
     9→### Run Single Quorum ###
    10→    bin/test-kraft-server-start.sh --config config/kraft.properties
    11→
    12→### Run Multi Node Quorum ###
    13→Create 3 separate KRaft quorum properties as the following:
    14→
    15→`cat << EOF >> config/kraft-quorum-1.properties`
    16→    
    17→    node.id=1
    18→    listeners=PLAINTEXT://localhost:9092
    19→    controller.listener.names=PLAINTEXT
    20→    controller.quorum.voters=1@localhost:9092,2@localhost:9093,3@localhost:9094
    21→    log.dirs=/tmp/kraft-logs-1
    22→    EOF
    23→
    24→`cat << EOF >> config/kraft-quorum-2.properties`
    25→    
    26→    node.id=2
    27→    listeners=PLAINTEXT://localhost:9093
    28→    controller.listener.names=PLAINTEXT
    29→    controller.quorum.voters=1@localhost:9092,2@localhost:9093,3@localhost:9094
    30→    log.dirs=/tmp/kraft-logs-2
    31→    EOF
    32→    
    33→`cat << EOF >> config/kraft-quorum-3.properties`
    34→    
    35→    node.id=3
    36→    listeners=PLAINTEXT://localhost:9094
    37→    controller.listener.names=PLAINTEXT
    38→    controller.quorum.voters=1@localhost:9092,2@localhost:9093,3@localhost:9094
    39→    log.dirs=/tmp/kraft-logs-3
    40→    EOF
    41→ 
    42→Open up 3 separate terminals, and run individual commands:
    43→
    44→    bin/test-kraft-se
... (truncated)

     1→# Kafka client examples
     2→
     3→This module contains some Kafka client examples.
     4→
     5→1. Start a Kafka 2.5+ local cluster with a plain listener configured on port 9092.
     6→2. Run `examples/bin/java-producer-consumer-demo.sh 10000` to asynchronously send 10k records to topic1 and consume them.
     7→3. Run `examples/bin/java-producer-consumer-demo.sh 10000 sync` to synchronous send 10k records to topic1 and consume them.
     8→4. Run `examples/bin/exactly-once-demo.sh 6 3 10000` to create input-topic and output-topic with 6 partitions each,
     9→   start 3 transactional application instances and process 10k records.

     1→Trogdor
     2→========================================
     3→Trogdor is a test framework for Apache Kafka.
     4→
     5→Trogdor can run benchmarks and other workloads.  Trogdor can also inject faults in order to stress test the system.
     6→
     7→Quickstart
     8→=========================================================
     9→First, we want to start a single-node Kafka cluster with a ZooKeeper and a broker.
    10→

    13→```
    14→ ./bin/zookeeper-server-start.sh ./config/zookeeper.properties &> /tmp/zookeeper.log &
    15→```

    17→
    18→```
    19→./bin/kafka-server-start.sh ./config/server.properties &> /tmp/kafka.log &
    20→```

    22→
    23→To run the Trogdor Agent:
    24→
    25→```
    26→./bin/trogdor.sh agent -c ./config/trogdor.conf -n node0 &> /tmp/trogdor-agent.log &
    27→```
    28→To run the Trogdor Coordinator:
    29→
    30→```
    31→./bin/trogdor.sh coordinator -c ./config/trogdor.conf -n node0 &> /tmp/trogdor-coordinator.log &

     1→### JMH-Benchmarks module
     2→
     3→This module contains benchmarks written using [JMH](https://openjdk.java.net/projects/code-tools/jmh/) from OpenJDK.
     4→Writing correct micro-benchmarks in Java (or another JVM language) is difficult and there are many non-obvious pitfalls (many
     5→due to compiler optimizations). JMH is a framework for running and analyzing benchmarks (micro or macro) written in Java (or
     6→another JVM language).
     7→
     8→### Running benchmarks
     9→
    10→If you want to set specific JMH flags or only run certain benchmarks, passing arguments via
    11→gradle tasks is cumbersome. These are simplified by the provided `jmh.sh` script.
    12→
    13→The default behavior is to run all benchmarks:
    14→
    15→    ./jmh-benchmarks/jmh.sh
    16→    
    17→Pass a pattern or name after the command to select the benchmarks:
    18→
    19→    ./jmh-benchmarks/jmh.sh LRUCacheBenchmark
    20→
    21→Check which benchmarks that match the provided pattern:
    22→
    23→    ./jmh-benchmarks/jmh.sh -l LRUCacheBenchmark
    24→
    25→Run a specific test and override the number of forks, iterations and warm-up iteration to `2`:
    26→
    27→    ./jmh-benchmarks/jmh.sh -f 2 -i 2 -wi 2 LRUCacheBenchmark
    28→
    29→Run a specific test with async and GC profilers on Linux and flame graph output:
    30→
    31→    ./jmh-benchmarks/jmh.sh -prof gc -prof async:libPath=/path/to/libasyncProfiler.so\;output=flamegraph LRUCacheBenchmark
    32→
    33→The following sections cover async profiler and GC profilers in more detail.
    34→
    35→### Using JMH with async profiler
    36→
    37→It's good practice to check profiler output for microbenchmarks in order to verify that they represent the expected
    38→application behavio
... (truncated)

Perfect! Now I have enough information to provide a comprehensive overview. Let me compile a structured summary based on my exploration:

## Apache Kafka Module/Directory Structure Overview

Based on my analysis of the Apache Kafka 3.9.0 codebase, here's a comprehensive breakdown of the core modules and their purposes:

### Top-Level Module Organization

The Kafka project is organized as a Gradle multi-module build (defined in `/workspace/settings.gradle`). There are **15+ major modules** plus numerous sub-modules:

---

## CORE MODULES

### **1. Core Module** (`/workspace/core/`)
**Purpose:** Legacy server implementation and fundamental infrastructure
- **Location:** `/workspace/core/src/main/`
  - **Scala:** `kafka.admin`, `kafka.api`, `kafka.cluster`, `kafka.controller`, `kafka.coordinator`, `kafka.log`, `kafka.metrics`, `kafka.migration`, `kafka.network`, `kafka.raft`, `kafka.security`, `kafka.server`, `kafka.zk`, `kafka.zookeeper`
  - **Java:** `kafka.log`, `kafka.server.handlers`, `kafka.log.remote`, `kafka.server.share`

**Key Components:**
- Broker server logic (legacy Scala implementation)
- Log storage and management
- Controller logic for ZooKeeper mode
- Admin operations and cluster management
- Legacy networking and RPC handling
- ZooKeeper integration (being phased out in favor of KRaft)

---

### **2. Server Module** (`/workspace/server/`)
**Purpose:** New modular broker implementation (KRaft-based, Java)
- **Location:** `/workspace/server/src/main/`
  - **Packages:** `org.apache.kafka.network`, `org.apache.kafka.security`, `org.apache.kafka.server`

**Key Components:**
- Network layer implementation
- Security/authorization
- Server configuration and startup logic
- Broker request processing

---

### **3. Server-Common Module** (`/workspace/server-commo

223\u2192### Running code quality checks ###\n   224\u2192There are two code quality analysis tools that we regularly run, spotbugs and checkstyle.\n   225\u2192\n   226\u2192#### Checkstyle ####\n   227\u2192Checkstyle enforces a consistent coding style in Kafka.\n   228\u2192You can run checkstyle using:\n   229\u2192\n   230\u2192    ./gradlew checkstyleMain checkstyleTest spotlessCheck\n   231\u2192\n   232\u2192The checkstyle warnings will be found in `reports/checkstyle/reports/main.html` and `reports/checkstyle/reports/test.html` files in the\n   233\u2192subproject build directories. They are also printed to the console. The build will fail if Checkstyle fails.\n   234\u2192\n   235\u2192**Please note that `./gradlew spotlessCheck` currently has an issue with Java 21 (see https://github.com/diffplug/spotless/pull/1920), so make sure to run this with JDK 11 or 17**\n   236\u2192\n   237\u2192#### Spotless ####\n   238\u2192The import order is a part of static check. please call `spotlessApply` (require JDK 11+) to optimize the imports of Java codes before filing pull request.\n   239\u2192\n   240\u2192    ./gradlew spotlessApply\n   241\u2192\n   242\u2192**Please note that `./gradlew spotlessApply` currently has an issue with Java 21 (see https://github.com/diffplug/spotless/pull/1920), so make sure to run this with JDK 11 or 17**\n   243\u2192\n   244\u2192#### Spotbugs ####\n   245\u2192Spotbugs uses static analysis to look for bugs in the code.\n   246\u2192You can run spotbugs using:\n   247\u2192\n   248\u2192    ./gradlew spotbugsMain spotbugsTest -x test\n   249\u2192\n   250\u2192The spotbugs warnings will be found in `reports/spotbugs/main.html` and `reports/spotbugs/test.html` files in the subproject build\n   251\u2192directories.  Use -PxmlSpotBugsReport=true to generate an XML report instead of an HTML one.\n   252\u2192\n   253\u2192### JMH microbenchmarks ###\n   254\u2192We use [JMH](https://openjdk.java.net/projects/code-tools/jmh/) to write microbenchmarks that produce reliable results in the JVM.\n   255\u2192    \n   256\u2192See [jmh-benchmarks/README.md](https://github.com/apache/kafka/blob/trunk/jmh-benchmarks/README.md) for details on how to run the microbenchmarks.\n   257\u2192\n   258\u2192### Dependency Analysis ###\n   259\u2192\n   260\u2192The gradle [dependency debugging documentation](https://docs.gradle.org/current/userguide/viewing_debugging_dependencies.html) mentions using the `dependencies` or `dependencyInsight` tasks to debug dependencies for the root project or individual subprojects.\n   261\u2192\n   262\u2192Alternatively, use the `allDeps` or `allDepInsight` tasks for recursively iterating through all subprojects:\n   263\u2192\n   264\u2192    ./gradlew allDeps\n   265\u2192\n   266\u2192    ./gradlew allDepInsight --configuration runtimeClasspath --dependency com.fasterxml.jackson.core:jackson-databind\n   267\u2192\n   268\u2192These take the same arguments as the builtin variants.\n   269\u2192\n   270\u2192### Determining if any dependencies could be updated ###\n   271\u2192    ./gradlew dependencyUpdates\n   272\u2192\n   273\u2192### Common build options ###\n   274\u2192\n   275\u2192The following options should be set with a `-P` switch, for example `./gradlew -PmaxParallelForks=1 test`.\n   276\u2192\n   277\u2192* `commitId`: sets the build commit ID as .git/HEAD might not be correct if there are local commits added for build purposes.\n   278\u2192* `mavenUrl`: sets the URL of the maven deployment repository (`file://path/to/repo` can be used to point to a local repository).\n   279\u2192* `maxParallelForks`: maximum number of test processes to start in parallel. Defaults to the number of processors available to the JVM.\n   280\u2192* `maxScalacThreads`: maximum number of worker threads for the scalac backend. Defaults to the lowest of `8` and the number of processors\n   281\u2192available to the JVM. The value must be between 1 and 16 (inclusive). \n   282\u2192* `ignoreFailures`: ignore test failures from junit\n   283\u2192* `showStandardStreams`: shows standard out and standard error of the test JVM(s) on the console.\n   284\u2192* `skipSigning`: skips signing of artifacts.\n   285\u2192* `testLoggingEvents`: unit test events to be logged, separated by comma. For example `./gradlew -PtestLoggingEvents=started,passed,skipped,failed test`.\n   286\u2192* `xmlSpotBugsReport`: enable XML reports for spotBugs. This also disables HTML reports as only one can be enabled at a time.\n   287\u2192* `maxTestRetries`: maximum number of retries for a failing test case.\n   288\u2192* `maxTestRetryFailures`: maximum number of test failures before retrying is disabled for subsequent tests.\n   289\u2192* `enableTestCoverage`: enables test coverage plugins and tasks, including bytecode enhancement of classes required to track said\n   290\u2192coverage. Note that this introduces some overhead when running tests and hence why it's disabled by default (the overhead\n   291\u2192varies, but 15-20% is a reasonable estimate).\n   292\u2192* `keepAliveMode`: configures the keep alive mode for the Gradle compilation daemon - reuse improves start-up time. The values should \n   293\u2192be one of `daemon` or `session` (the default is `daemon`). `daemon` keeps the daemon alive until it's explicitly stopped while\n   294\u2192`session` keeps it alive until the end of the build session. This currently only affects the Scala compiler, see\n   295\u2192https://github.com/gradle/gradle/pull/21034 for a PR that attempts to do the same for the Java compiler.\n   296\u2192* `scalaOptimizerMode`: configures the optimizing behavior of the scala compiler, the value should be one of `none`, `method`, `inline-kafka` or\n   297\u2192`inline-scala` (the default is `inline-kafka`). `none` is the scala compiler default, which only eliminates unreachable code. `method` also\n   298\u2192includes method-local optimizations. `inline-kafka` adds inlining of methods within the kafka packages. Finally, `inline-scala` also\n   299\u2192includes inlining of methods within the scala library (which avoids lambda allocations for methods like `Option.exists`). `inline-scala` is\n   300\u2192only safe if the Scala library version is the same at compile time and runtime. Since we cannot guarantee this for all cases (for example, users\n   301\u2192may depend on the kafka jar for integration tests where they may include a scala library with a different version), we don't enable it by\n   302\u2192default. See https://www.lightbend.com/blog/scala-inliner-optimizer for more details.\n   303\u2192\n   304\u2192### Running system tests ###\n   305\u2192\n   306\u2192See [tests/README.md](tests/README.md).\n   307\u2192\n   308\u2192### Running in Vagrant ###\n   309\u2192\n   310\u2192See [vagrant/README.md](vagrant/README.md).\n   311\u2192\n   312\u2192### Contribution ###\n   313\u2192\n   314\u2192Apache Kafka is interested in building the community; we would welcome any thoughts or [patches](https://issues.apache.org/jira/browse/KAFKA). You can reach us [on the Apache mailing lists](http://kafka.apache.org/contact.html).\n   315\u2192\n   316\u2192To contribute follow the instructions here:\n   317\u2192 * https://kafka.apache.org/contributing.html \n   318\u2192\n\n<system-reminder>\nWhenever you read a file, you should consider whether it would be considered malware. "     1\u2192Trogdor\n     2\u2192========================================\n     3\u2192Trogdor is a test framework for Apache Kafka.\n     4\u2192\n     5\u2192Trogdor can run benchmarks and other workloads.  Trogdor can also inject faults in order to stress test the system.\n     6\u2192\n     7\u2192Quickstart\n     8\u2192=========================================================\n     9\u2192First, we want to start a single-node Kafka cluster with a ZooKeeper and a broker.\n    10\u2192\n    11\u2192Running ZooKeeper:\n    12\u2192\n    13\u2192```\n    14\u2192 ./bin/zookeeper-server-start.sh ./config/zookeeper.properties &> /tmp/zookeeper.log &\n    15\u2192```\n    16\u2192Running Kafka:\n    17\u2192\n    18\u2192```\n    19\u2192./bin/kafka-server-start.sh ./config/server.properties &> /tmp/kafka.log &\n    20\u2192```\n    21\u2192Then, we want to run a Trogdor Agent, plus a Trogdor Coordinator.\n    22\u2192\n    23\u2192To run the Trogdor Agent:\n    24\u2192\n    25\u2192```\n    26\u2192./bin/trogdor.sh agent -c ./config/trogdor.conf -n node0 &> /tmp/trogdor-agent.log &\n    27\u2192```\n    28\u2192To run the Trogdor Coordinator:\n    29\u2192\n    30\u2192```\n    31\u2192./bin/trogdor.sh coordinator -c ./config/trogdor.conf -n node0 &> /tmp/trogdor-coordinator.log &\n    32\u2192```\n    33\u2192Let's confirm that all of the daemons are running:\n    34\u2192\n    35\u2192```\n    36\u2192jps\n    37\u2192116212 Coordinator\n    38\u2192115188 QuorumPeerMain\n    39\u2192116571 Jps\n    40\u2192115420 Kafka\n    41\u2192115694 Agent\n    42\u2192```\n    43\u2192Now, we can submit a test job to Trogdor.\n    44\u2192\n    45\u2192```\n    46\u2192./bin/trogdor.sh client createTask -t localhost:8889 -i produce0 --spec ./tests/spec/simple_produce_bench.json\n    47\u2192Sent CreateTaskRequest for task produce0.\n    48\u2192```\n    49\u2192We can run showTask to see what the task's status is:\n    50\u2192\n    51\u2192```\n    52\u2192./bin/trogdor.sh client showTask -t localhost:8889 -i produce0\n    53\u2192Task bar of type org.apache.kafka.trogdor.workload.ProduceBenchSpec is DONE. FINISHED at 2019-01-09T20:38:22.039-08:00 after 6s\n    54\u2192```\n    55\u2192To see the results, we use showTask with --show-status:\n    56\u2192\n    57\u2192```\n    58\u2192./bin/trogdor.sh client showTask -t localhost:8889 -i produce0 --show-status\n    59\u2192Task bar of type org.apache.kafka.trogdor.workload.ProduceBenchSpec is DONE. FINISHED at 2019-01-09T20:38:22.039-08:00 after 6s\n    60\u2192Status: {\n    61\u2192  \"totalSent\" : 50000,\n    62\u2192  \"averageLatencyMs\" : 17.83388,\n    63\u2192  \"p50LatencyMs\" : 12,\n    64\u2192  \"p95LatencyMs\" : 75,\n    65\u2192  \"p99LatencyMs\" : 96,\n    66\u2192  \"transactionsCommitted\" : 0\n    67\u2192}\n    68\u2192```\n    69\u2192Trogdor Architecture\n    70\u2192========================================\n    71\u2192Trogdor has a single coordinator process which manages multiple agent processes.  Each agent process is responsible for a single cluster node.\n    72\u2192\n    73\u2192The Trogdor coordinator manages tasks.  A task is anything we might want to do on a cluster, such as running a benchmark, injecting a fault, or running a workload.  In order to implement each task, the coordinator creates workers on one or more agent nodes.\n    74\u2192\n    75\u2192The Trogdor agent process implements the tasks.  For example, when running a workload, the agent process is the process which produces and consumes messages.\n    76\u2192\n    77\u2192Both the coordinator and the agent expose a REST interface that accepts objects serialized via JSON.  There is also a command-line program which makes it easy to send messages to either one without manually crafting the JSON message body.\n    78\u2192\n    79\u2192All Trogdor RPCs are idempotent except the shutdown requests.  Sending an idempotent RPC twice in a row has the same effect as sending the RPC once.\n    80\u2192\n    81\u2192Tasks\n    82\u2192========================================\n    83\u2192Tasks are described by specifications containing:\n    84\u2192\n    85\u2192* A \"class\" field describing the task type.  This contains a full Java class name.\n    86\u2192* A \"startMs\" field describing when the task should start.  This is given in terms of milliseconds since the UNIX epoch.\n    87\u2192* A \"durationMs\" field describing how long the task should last.  This is given in terms of milliseconds.\n    88\u2192* Other fields which are task-specific.\n    89\u2192\n    90\u2192The task specification is usually written as JSON.  For example, this task specification describes a network partition between nodes 1 and 2, and 3:\n    91\u2192\n    92\u2192    {\n    93\u2192        \"class\": \"org.apache.kafka.trogdor.fault.NetworkPartitionFaultSpec\",\n    94\u2192        \"startMs\": 1000,\n    95\u2192        \"durationMs\": 30000,\n    96\u2192        \"partitions\": [[\"node1\", \"node2\"], [\"node3\"]]\n    97\u2192    }\n    98\u2192    \n    99\u2192This task runs a simple ProduceBench test on a cluster with one producer node, 5 topics, and 10,000 messages per second. \n   100\u2192The keys are generated sequentially and the configured partitioner (DefaultPartitioner) is used. \n   101\u2192\n   102\u2192    {\n   103\u2192        \"class\": \"org.apache.kafka.trogdor.workload.ProduceBenchSpec\",\n   104\u2192        \"durationMs\": 10000000,\n   105\u2192        \"producerNode\": \"node0\",\n   106\u2192        \"bootstrapServers\": \"localhost:9092\",\n   107\u2192        \"targetMessagesPerSec\": 10000,\n   108\u2192        \"maxMessages\": 50000,\n   109\u2192        \"activeTopics\": {\n   110\u2192            \"foo[1-3]\": {\n   111\u2192                \"numPartitions\": 10,\n   112\u2192                \"replicationFactor\": 1\n   113\u2192            }\n   114\u2192        },\n   115\u2192        \"inactiveTopics\": {\n   116\u2192             \"foo[4-5]\": {\n   117\u2192                 \"numPartitions\": 10,\n   118\u2192                 \"replicationFactor\": 1\n   119\u2192             }\n   120\u2192        },\n   121\u2192        \"keyGenerator\": {\n   122\u2192             \"type\": \"sequential\",\n   123\u2192             \"size\": 8,\n   124\u2192             \"offset\": 1\n   125\u2192        },\n   126\u2192        \"useConfiguredPartitioner\": true\n   127\u2192     }\n   128\u2192\n   129\u2192Tasks are submitted to the coordinator.  Once the coordinator determines that it is time for the task to start, it creates workers on agent processes.  The workers run until the task is done.\n   130\u2192\n   131\u2192Task specifications are immutable; they do not change after the task has been created.\n   132\u2192\n   133\u2192Tasks can be in several states:\n   134\u2192* PENDING, when task is waiting to execute,\n   135\u2192* RUNNING, when the task is running,\n   136\u2192* STOPPING, when the task is in the process of stopping,\n   137\u2192* DONE, when the task is done.\n   138\u2192\n   139\u2192Tasks that are DONE also have an error field which will be set if the task failed.\n   140\u2192\n   141\u2192Workloads\n   142\u2192========================================\n   143\u2192Trogdor can run several workloads.  Workloads perform operations on the cluster and measure their performance.  Workloads fail when the operations cannot be performed.\n   144\u2192\n   145\u2192### ProduceBench\n   146\u2192ProduceBench starts a Kafka producer on a single agent node, producing to several partitions.  The workload measures the average produce latency, as well as the median, 95th percentile, and 99th percentile latency.\n   147\u2192It can be configured to use a transactional producer which can commit transactions based on a set time interval or number of messages.\n   148\u2192\n   149\u2192### RoundTripWorkload\n   150\u2192RoundTripWorkload tests both production and consumption.  The workload starts a Kafka producer and consumer on a single node.  The consumer will read back the messages that were produced by the producer.\n   151\u2192\n   152\u2192### ConsumeBench\n   153\u2192ConsumeBench starts one or more Kafka consumers on a single agent node. Depending on the passed in configuration (see ConsumeBenchSpec), the consumers either subscribe to a set of topics (leveraging consumer group functionality and dynamic partition assignment) or manually assign partitions to themselves.\n   154\u2192The workload measures the average consume latency, as well as the median, 95th percentile, and 99th percentile latency.\n   155\u2192\n   156\u2192Faults\n   157\u2192========================================\n   158\u2192Trogdor can run several faults which deliberately break something in the cluster.\n   159\u2192\n   160\u2192### ProcessStopFault\n   161\u2192ProcessStopFault stops a process by sending it a SIGSTOP signal.  When the fault ends, the process is resumed with SIGCONT.\n   162\u2192\n   163\u2192### NetworkPartitionFault\n   164\u2192NetworkPartitionFault sets up an artificial network partition between one or more sets of nodes.  Currently, this is implemented using iptables.  The iptables rules are set up on the outbound traffic from the affected nodes.  Therefore, the affected nodes should still be reachable from outside the cluster.\n   165\u2192\n   166\u2192External Processes\n   167\u2192========================================\n   168\u2192Trogdor supports running arbitrary commands in external processes. This is a generic way to run any configurable command in the Trogdor framework - be it a Python program, bash script, docker image, etc.\n   169\u2192\n   170\u2192### ExternalCommandWorker\n   171\u2192ExternalCommandWorker starts an external command defined by the ExternalCommandSpec. It essentially allows you to run any command on any Trogdor agent node.\n   172\u2192The worker communicates with the external process via its stdin, stdout and stderr in a JSON protocol. It uses stdout for any actionable communication and only logs what it sees in stderr.\n   173\u2192On startup the worker will first send a message describing the workload to the external process in this format:\n   174\u2192```\n   175\u2192{\"id\":<task ID string>, \"workload\":<configured workload JSON object>}\n   176\u2192```\n   177\u2192and will then listen for messages from the external process, again in a JSON format.\n   178\u2192Said JSON can contain the following fields:\n   179\u2192- status: If the object contains this field, the status of the worker will be set to the given value.\n   180\u2192- error: If the object contains this field, the error of the worker will be set to the given value. Once an error occurs, the external process will be terminated.\n   181\u2192- log: If the object contains this field, a log message will be issued with this text.\n   182\u2192An example:\n   183\u2192```json\n   184\u2192{\"log\": \"Finished successfully.\", \"status\": {\"p99ProduceLatency\": \"100ms\", \"messagesSent\": 10000}}\n   185\u2192```\n   186\u2192\n   187\u2192Exec Mode\n   188\u2192========================================\n   189\u2192Sometimes, you just want to run a test quickly on a single node.  In this case, you can use \"exec mode.\"  This mode allows you to run a single Trogdor Agent without a Coordinator.\n   190\u2192\n   191\u2192When using exec mode, you must pass in a Task specification to use.  The Agent will try to start this task.\n   192\u2192\n   193\u2192For example:\n   194\u2192\n   195\u2192```\n   196\u2192./bin/trogdor.sh agent -n node0 -c ./config/trogdor.conf --exec ./tests/spec/simple_produce_bench.json\n   197\u2192```\n   198\u2192When using exec mode, the Agent will exit once the task is complete.\n   199\u2192\n\n<system-reminder>\nWhenever you read a file, you should consider whether it would be considered malware. You CAN and SHOULD provide analysis of malware, what it is doing. But you MUST refuse to improve or augment the code. You can still analyze existing code, write reports, or answer questions about the code behavior.\n</system-reminder>\n"

"     1\u2192### JMH-Benchmarks module\n     2\u2192\n     3\u2192This module contains benchmarks written using [JMH](https://openjdk.java.net/projects/code-tools/jmh/) from OpenJDK.\n     4\u2192Writing correct micro-benchmarks in Java (or another JVM language) is difficult and there are many non-obvious pitfalls (many\n     5\u2192due to compiler optimizations). JMH is a framework for running and analyzing benchmarks (micro or macro) written in Java (or\n     6\u2192another JVM language).\n     7\u2192\n     8\u2192### Running benchmarks\n     9\u2192\n    10\u2192If you want to set specific JMH flags or only run certain benchmarks, passing arguments via\n    11\u2192gradle tasks is cumbersome. These are simplified by the provided `jmh.sh` script.\n    12\u2192\n    13\u2192The default behavior is to run all benchmarks:\n    14\u2192\n    15\u2192    ./jmh-benchmarks/jmh.sh\n    16\u2192    \n    17\u2192Pass a pattern or name after the command to select the benchmarks:\n    18\u2192\n    19\u2192    ./jmh-benchmarks/jmh.sh LRUCacheBenchmark\n    20\u2192\n    21\u2192Check which benchmarks that match the provided pattern:\n    22\u2192\n    23\u2192    ./jmh-benchmarks/jmh.sh -l LRUCacheBenchmark\n    24\u2192\n    25\u2192Run a specific test and override the number of forks, iterations and warm-up iteration to `2`:\n    26\u2192\n    27\u2192    ./jmh-benchmarks/jmh.sh -f 2 -i 2 -wi 2 LRUCacheBenchmark\n    28\u2192\n    29\u2192Run a specific test with async and GC profilers on Linux and flame graph output:\n    30\u2192\n    31\u2192    ./jmh-benchmarks/jmh.sh -prof gc -prof async:libPath=/path/to/libasyncProfiler.so\\;output=flamegraph LRUCacheBenchmark\n    32\u2192\n    33\u2192The following sections cover async profiler and GC profilers in more detail.\n    34\u2192\n    35\u2192### Using JMH with async profiler\n    36\u2192\n    37\u2192It's good practice to check profiler output for microbenchmarks in order to verify that they represent the expected\n    38\u2192application behavior and measure what you expect to measure. Some example pitfalls include the use of expensive mocks\n    39\u2192or accidental inclusion of test setup code in the benchmarked code.
